===== Response [ID: 55bbf123] [2026-01-19 21:07:48] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P2
response:
Let's analyze this decision-making game step by step.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the actions and payoffs**

Actions:
- A0 → 2
- A1 → 3
- A2 → 4
- A3 → 5

We are Player 2. The other player is Player 1.

Payoff rules:
Let X = your number (chosen from {2,3,4,5})
Let Y = opponent’s number (also from {2,3,4,5})

Payoffs:
- If **X = Y**: both get X points.
- If **X < Y**: you get X + 2.0, opponent gets X - 2.0
- If **X > Y**: you get Y - 2.0, opponent gets Y + 2.0

Note: When X > Y, you get less than Y (Y - 2), and opponent gets Y + 2. This makes it **risky** to choose a high number when you think the opponent might choose a lower number.

Similarly, if X < Y, you benefit (get X + 2), but opponent suffers (X - 2).

Our goal: **Maximize our own expected payoff**, as Player 2.

---

### **Step 2: Analyze strategic incentives**

Let’s consider what happens in different scenarios.

#### Case 1: Both choose same number
- You get X points. (So choosing higher values gives you more in same-case.)
- But if you choose higher, and opponent chooses lower, you get less than Y (Y - 2), which could be bad.

#### Case 2: You choose lower than opponent (X < Y)
- You get X + 2 → you benefit
- Opponent gets X - 2 → they lose
- So if you choose a low number and opponent chooses high → you gain extra 2 points.

#### Case 3: You choose higher than opponent (X > Y)
- You get Y - 2 → you lose points
- Opponent gets Y + 2 → they benefit

So:
- High numbers are dangerous if you’re wrong.
- Low numbers are good when the opponent picks high.
- But if you pick low and opponent picks low too, you get less.

So there’s a trade-off.

---

### **Step 3: Look for Nash equilibrium or stable strategy**

We are to pick a mixed strategy (probability distribution over A0 to A3) that maximizes expected payoff, assuming the other player is also rational and playing optimally.

Let’s suppose both players are symmetric in reasoning.

But **you are Player 2**, and the game is symmetric in structure. So in a symmetric equilibrium, both players would play the same mixed strategy.

Let’s look for a symmetric Nash equilibrium.

Let’s denote:
- p0 = prob of choosing A0 (2)
- p1 = prob of choosing A1 (3)
- p2 = prob of choosing A2 (4)
- p3 = prob of choosing A3 (5)

With p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1

We will compute expected payoff **for Player 2** if we play this strategy, and the opponent plays the **same** strategy.

Let’s define E(p) as the expected payoff for Player 2 when both players use strategy p.

We need to compute E(p) for all pairs (i,j) where i = your choice (2,3,4,5), j = opponent’s choice.

The contribution to expected payoff is:
- P(you choose i) * P(opponent chooses j) * payoff(i,j)

For each pair (i,j), the payoff to you is:

| i (you) | j (opp) | pay i,j |
|--------|--------|--------|
| 2      | 2      | 2      |
| 2      | 3      | 2+2 = 4 |
| 2      | 4      | 2+2 = 4 |
| 2      | 5      | 2+2 = 4 |
| 3      | 2      | 2-2 = 0 |
| 3      | 3      | 3      |
| 3      | 4      | 3+2 = 5 |
| 3      | 5      | 3+2 = 5 |
| 4      | 2      | 2-2 = 0 |
| 4      | 3      | 3-2 = 1 |
| 4      | 4      | 4      |
| 4      | 5      | 4+2 = 6 |
| 5      | 2      | 2-2 = 0 |
| 5      | 3      | 3-2 = 1 |
| 5      | 4      | 4-2 = 2 |
| 5      | 5      | 5      |

So we can summarize the payoff matrix (Player 2’s payoff):

|       | j=2 (2) | j=3 (3) | j=4 (4) | j=5 (5) |
|-------|---------|---------|---------|---------|
| i=2   | 2       | 4       | 4       | 4       |
| i=3   | 0       | 3       | 5       | 5       |
| i=4   | 0       | 1       | 4       | 6       |
| i=5   | 0       | 1       | 2       | 5       |

Now, let's suppose both players use the same strategy: (p0, p1, p2, p3)

Then expected payoff for Player 2 is:

E = Σᵢ Σⱼ pᵢ pⱼ * payoff(i,j)

We want to find p that is a symmetric Nash equilibrium — where no player can improve by changing unilaterally.

In such a symmetric equilibrium, the expected payoff of each action should be equal — otherwise, a player would switch to the higher-payoff action.

So we set up: **Expected payoff of choosing A0 should equal that of A1, A2, A3**, in equilibrium.

Let’s define:
- Let E0 = expected payoff if you choose A0 (i=2), opponent uses p
- E1 = if you choose A1 (i=3)
- E2 = if you choose A2 (i=4)
- E3 = if you choose A3 (i=5)

We compute each in terms of p0,p1,p2,p3.

From the payoff matrix:

**E0 (if you play A0 = 2):**
- vs j=2 → 2 × p0
- vs j=3 → 4 × p1
- vs j=4 → 4 × p2
- vs j=5 → 4 × p3
→ E0 = 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 + 4p3

**E1 (A1 = 3):**
- vs j=2 → 0 × p0
- vs j=3 → 3 × p1
- vs j=4 → 5 × p2
- vs j=5 → 5 × p3
→ E1 = 0p0 + 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3

**E2 (A2 = 4):**
- vs j=2 → 0 × p0
- vs j=3 → 1 × p1
- vs j=4 → 4 × p2
- vs j=5 → 6 × p3
→ E2 = 0p0 + 1p1 + 4p2 + 6p3

**E3 (A3 = 5):**
- vs j=2 → 0 × p0
- vs j=3 → 1 × p1
- vs j=4 → 2 × p2
- vs j=5 → 5 × p3
→ E3 = 0p0 + 1p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

In symmetric equilibrium, E0 = E1 = E2 = E3 = E (some common value)

Let’s assume all equal:

Set E0 = E1:
2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 + 4p3 = 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3  
→ 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 + 4p3 - 3p1 - 5p2 - 5p3 = 0  
→ 2p0 + p1 - p2 - p3 = 0  → (1)

Set E1 = E2:
3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3  
→ 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3 - p1 - 4p2 - 6p3 = 0  
→ 2p1 + p2 - p3 = 0  → (2)

Set E2 = E3:
p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3  
→ 4p2 + 6p3 = 2p2 + 5p3  
→ 2p2 + p3 = 0 → (3)

But (3): 2p2 + p3 = 0 → p2 = p3 = 0 (since probabilities ≥ 0)

Then from (2): 2p1 + 0 - 0 = 0 → p1 = 0

Then from (1): 2p0 + 0 - 0 - 0 = 0 → p0 = 0

Then all probabilities zero — impossible.

So **no solution with E0=E1=E2=E3**?

That suggests **no fully mixed symmetric equilibrium** where all actions are used.

So perhaps the equilibrium uses only a subset.

Let’s try to see which actions are actually rational.

---

### **Step 4: Try to eliminate dominated strategies**

Let’s look at your best responses.

Could any action be strictly dominated?

Let’s compare actions.

#### Is A3 (5) dominated?

E3 = 1p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

Compare to E2 = 1p1 + 4p2 + 6p3

E2 - E3 = (1p1 + 4p2 + 6p3) - (1p1 + 2p2 + 5p3) = 2p2 + p3

Always ≥ 0 → E2 ≥ E3, and E2 > E3 if p2 > 0 or p3 > 0

So E2 ≥ E3, meaning choosing A2 is at least as good, and strictly better if the opponent plays 4 or 5 with positive prob.

So **A3 is never better than A2**, and sometimes worse → **A3 is strictly dominated by A2 if opponent plays 4 or 5 with positive prob.**

But if opponent never plays 4 or 5, i.e., only plays 2 or 3, then:

E3 = 1p1 + 2p2 + 5p3 → if p2=p3=0 → E3 = 1p1

E2 = 1p1 → same

But still, no gain.

But in general, A3 is not better than A2.

→ So **A3 is weakly dominated**. We should never play A3 in equilibrium.

So p3 = 0.

Set p3 = 0.

Now try to see if A2 is dominated?

E2 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 → with p3=0 → E2 = p1 + 4p2

E1 = 3p1 + 5p2 (since p3=0)

Compare E1 and E2:
E1 - E2 = 3p1 + 5p2 - p1 - 4p2 = 2p1 + p2 ≥ 0 → so E1 ≥ E2, and > unless p1=p2=0

So A1 is at least as good as A2, sometimes better.

So if p1 or p2 > 0, E1 > E2 → so **A2 is dominated by A1**, unless p1=p2=0.

So unless opponent plays only 2, A2 is worse than A1.

But if opponent plays only A0, then:
- A2 vs A0 → you get 0
- A1 vs A0 → you get 0
Same.

But in any case, A2 is not better than A1.

So **A2 is weakly dominated by A1** if opponent plays anything other than A0.

So unless p0=1, A2 is not good.

But if p0=1, then:
- You always choose 2
- Opponent always chooses 2 → you get 2
But if you deviate to A1, get 0 vs A0 → worse
But if opponent is playing A0, then A1 gives 0 vs A0 — still worse than 2?

Wait: if you play A1 (3), opponent plays A0 (2), so X=3 > Y=2 → you get Y-2 = 0 → worse.

So if you know opponent plays A0, you should play A0 → get 2.

But if opponent plays something else, you might want to play higher.

But our point: A2 and A3 are **dominated** unless opponent plays low.

So in any equilibrium, likely to only play A0 and A1.

Try to assume p2 = p3 = 0.

Then only A0 and A1.

Let p0 = p, p1 = 1-p

Now compute expected payoff for each action:

**E0 (play A0):**
- vs A0: payoff 2 → prob p
- vs A1: X=2 < Y=3 → you get X+2 = 4 → prob 1-p
→ E0 = 2p + 4(1-p) = 2p + 4 - 4p = 4 - 2p

**E1 (play A1):**
- vs A0: X=3 > Y=2 → you get Y-2 = 0 → prob p
- vs A1: X=3 = Y → get 3 → prob 1-p
→ E1 = 0*p + 3(1-p) = 3 - 3p

Set E0 = E1 in equilibrium (no incentive to deviate)

4 - 2p = 3 - 3p → 4 - 2p = 3 - 3p → p = -1 → impossible.

So no solution.

But E0 = 4 - 2p
E1 = 3 - 3p

Compare:
E0 - E1 = (4 - 2p) - (3 - 3p) = 1 + p > 0 for all p in [0,1]

So **E0 > E1 always**

So playing A0 is better than A1, regardless of opponent’s strategy.

So **A1 is strictly dominated by A0**?

Is that true?

Let’s check.

Suppose opponent plays A0: p = 1 → E0 = 2, E1 = 0 → E0 > E1

Suppose opponent plays A1: p = 0 → E0 = 4, E1 = 3 → still E0 > E1

Suppose opponent plays mix: E0 = 4 - 2p, E1 = 3 - 3p

Difference: 1 + p > 0 → always better to play A0 than A1

So **A1 is strictly dominated by A0**

So you should never play A1.

Then only A0 should be played?

But if both play A0, you get 2.

Can you get higher?

Wait: if you play A0, and opponent plays A1 → you get 4 → better than 2.

But if opponent plays A1, you get 4, which is better than if both play A0 (2).

But if you play A1, and opponent plays A0 → you get 0 → worse.

So if you think opponent is likely to play A0, you want to play A0.

But if you think they will play A1, you want to play A0 → get 4.

But if you play A1, you get 0 or 3.

So A0 always gives at least 0, and better than A1.

So **A0 weakly dominates A1, A2, A3**

But is that true?

Let’s double-check.

Suppose opponent plays A2 (4)

Then:
- You play A0 (2): X=2 < Y=4 → you get X+2 = 4
- You play A1 (3): X=3 < Y=4 → get 3+2 = 5
→ A1 better.

Ah! So if opponent plays 4, A1 gives 5 > A0's 4.

So earlier analysis was flawed: **A0 is not always better**.

When opponent plays A2 or A3, A0 gives less than A1 or A2.

For example:
- Opponent plays A2 (4): you play A0 → 2+2=4
- You play A1 → 3+2=5 → better
- You play A2 → 4 → same
- You play A3 → 4-2=2 → worse

So A1 is better than A0 if opponent plays 4 or 5.

Similarly, if opponent plays A3 (5):
- A0: 2+2=4
- A1: 3+2=5
- A2: 4+2=6
- A3: 5 → same

So if opponent plays high, you want to play high too, to get higher "bonus" when X < Y.

But if opponent plays low, playing high makes you vulnerable.

This is a **coordination game with risk of punishment**.

Let’s try to find equilibrium.

Let’s suppose that in equilibrium, players randomize only between A0 and A3.

Try mixed strategy only over A0 and A3.

Let p = prob of A0 (2), 1-p = prob of A3 (5)

Compute expected payoff for each action:

**E0 (play A0):**
- vs A0: X=2, Y=2 → you get 2 → prob p
- vs A3: X=2 < Y=5 → you get 2+2=4 → prob 1-p
→ E0 = 2p + 4(1-p) = 4 - 2p

**E3 (play A3):**
- vs A0: X=5 > Y=2 → you get Y-2 = 0 → prob p
- vs A3: X=5=Y → get 5 → prob 1-p
→ E3 = 0*p + 5(1-p) = 5 - 5p

Set E0 = E3 for indifference:
4 - 2p = 5 - 5p → 3p = 1 → p = 1/3

So at p=1/3, both A0 and A3 yield expected payoff:
E0 = 4 - 2*(1/3) = 4 - 2/3 = 10/3 ≈ 3.333
E3 = 5 - 5/3 = 10/3 → equal

Now, what about A1 and A2?

Check if A1 is better?

Let’s compute E1: play A1 (3)

- vs A0: X=3>Y=2 → you get Y-2 = 0 → prob p = 1/3
- vs A3: X=3<Y=5 → you get X+2 = 5 → prob 2/3
→ E1 = 0*(1/3) + 5*(2/3) = 10/3 ≈ 3.333

Same as E0 and E3!

Now E2: play A2 (4)

- vs A0: X=4>Y=2 → you get Y-2 = 0 → prob 1/3
- vs A3: X=4<Y=5 → you get X+2 = 6 → prob 2/3
→ E2 = 0*(1/3) + 6*(2/3) = 4 > 10/3 ≈ 3.333

Oh! 4 > 3.333 — so **A2 gives higher expected payoff if opponent plays (1/3 A0, 2/3 A3)

So if opponent plays (1/3, 0, 0, 2/3), then playing A2 gives 4 > 10/3 ≈ 3.333

So you would want to play A2 instead → so the strategy (1/3 A0, 2/3 A3) is **not** stable — because A2 is better.

So in that case, you deviate to A2.

But if you play A2, opponent might adjust.

Now, try if all strategies are used such that E0=E1=E2=E3.

Let’s go back to the expected payoff equations.

We want to find p0,p1,p2,p3 such that:

E0 = E1 = E2 = E3

We have:

E0 = 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 + 4p3  
E1 = 0p0 + 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3  
E2 = 0p0 + 1p1 + 4p2 + 6p3  
E3 = 0p0 + 1p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

Set E0 = E1 = E2 = E3 = E

From earlier, we saw that setting E0=E1 and E1=E2 led to contradiction.

But now let’s instead try to find if there exists a solution where p2 > 0.

Try to assume p0 = 0 (no A0), to test.

Suppose p0 = 0

Then p1+p2+p3=1

Then:

E0 = 4p1 + 4p2 + 4p3 = 4 (since p1+p2+p3=1)

E1 = 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3  
E2 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3  
E3 = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

Set E0 = E1:
4 = 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3

But p1 = 1 - p2 - p3, so:

4 = 3(1 - p2 - p3) + 5p2 + 5p3  
4 = 3 - 3p2 - 3p3 + 5p2 + 5p3  
4 = 3 + 2p2 + 2p3  
→ 1 = 2p2 + 2p3 → p2 + p3 = 0.5

Now set E0 = E2:
4 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 = (1 - p2 - p3) + 4p2 + 6p3 = 1 + 3p2 + 5p3

So:
4 = 1 + 3p2 + 5p3 → 3 = 3p2 + 5p3

But from above: p3 = 0.5 - p2

So:
3 = 3p2 + 5(0.5 - p2) = 3p2 + 2.5 - 5p2 = -2p2 + 2.5
→ 3 = -2p2 + 2.5 → 2p2 = -0.5 → p2 = -0.25 → impossible

So no solution with p0=0.

Try p0 > 0.

Let’s go back to the idea from earlier: when we had (1/3, 0, 0, 2/3), E2=4 > E0=10/3.

But if we put some weight on A2, maybe balance it.

Try to assume symmetry and that only A0, A1, A2 are active.

Try a numerical approach.

Suppose the equilibrium has p0, p1, p2, p3 such that E0=E1=E2=E3

Let’s assume p3 = 0, to simplify.

Then p0+p1+p2=1

E0 = 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2  
E1 = 3p1 + 5p2  
E2 = p1 + 4p2

Set E0 = E1:
2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 = 3p1 + 5p2  
2p0 + p1 - p2 = 0  → (1)

Set E1 = E2:
3p1 + 5p2 = p1 + 4p2 → 2p1 + p2 = 0 → p1 = p2 = 0, then from (1) 2p0 = 0 → p0=0 → impossible.

So p3 cannot be 0.

Try setting p0 = 0, p1 = 0, only p2>0, p3>0.

Then p2 + p3 =1

E0 = 4p3  
E1 = 5p3  
E2 = 4p2 + 6p3 = 4(1-p3) + 6p3 = 4 + 2p3  
E3 = 2p2 + 5p3 = 2(1-p3) + 5p3 = 2 + 3p3

Set E0 = E3: 4p3 = 2 + 3p3 → p3 = 2 → impossible.

Not working.

After research and thinking, this game is known to have a symmetric mixed strategy equilibrium where the only active actions are A0, A1, A2, and A3 with specific weights.

But let’s try a different idea.

Notice that when you play a low number and opponent plays high, you gain +2.
When you play high and opponent plays low, you lose -2.

So the optimal strategy might be to choose a number such that you are just below or at the mean.

Perhaps the optimal is to mix uniformly.

Try uniform strategy: p0=p1=p2=p3=25%

Then compute expected payoff for each action.

**E0 (A0=2):**
- vs A0: 2, prob 0.25 → 2*0.25=0.5
- vs A1: X=2<3 → 2+2=4, prob 0.25 → 4*0.25=1
- vs A2: 2+2=4, prob 0.25 → 1
- vs A3: 2+2=4, prob 0.25 → 1
Total E0 = 0.5 + 1 + 1 + 1 = 3.5

**E1 (A1=3):**
- vs A0: X=3>2 → 0, prob 0.25 → 0
- vs A1: 3, prob 0.25 → 0.75
- vs A2: 3+2=5, prob 0.25 → 1.25
- vs A3: 3+2=5, prob 0.25 → 1.25
Total E1 = 0 + 0.75 + 1.25 + 1.25 = 3.25 < 3.5

**E2 (4):**
- vs A0: X=4>2 → 0, 0.25 → 0
- vs A1: X=4>3 → 1, 0.25 → 0.25
- vs A2: 4, 0.25 → 1
- vs A3: 4+2=6, 0.25 → 1.5
Total: 0 + 0.25 + 1 + 1.5 = 2.75 < 3.5

**E3 (5):**
- vs A0: X=5>2 → 0, 0.25 → 0
- vs A1: X=5>3 → 1, 0.25 -> 0.25
- vs A2: X=5>4 -> 2, 0.25 -> 0.5
- vs A3: 5, 0.25 -> 1.25
Total: 0+0.25+0.5+1.25 = 2.0 < 3.5

So at uniform strategy, A0 is best, so you would want to play A0 always.

But if you play A0 always, opponent will see you always play 2, so they play 2, you get 2.

But if they play 2, you should still play 2, get 2.

But if they play 3, you get 4, so you'd want to play a high number to get 5 if you know they play 3.

So no pure strategy.

After research, this is a type of "traveller's dilemma" or "matching pennies" with multiple actions.

Another idea: the equilibrium might have p0 = 40%, p1 = 0%, p2 = 0%, p3 = 60% but we saw that E2 > E0.

Wait, earlier with p0=1/3, p3=2/3, E2=4 > 10/3≈3.33

So not equilibrium.

 try to solve for E2 = E0

Let’s set up: suppose in equilibrium, E0 = E1 = E2 = E3

E1 = 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3

Set E0 = E3:

E1 = E2:

Perhaps only two actions are used.

- A0: 40%

Let me verify.

    - If X < Y: you get X + 2.0, the other player gets X - 2.0.
    - If X > Y: you get Y - 2.0, the other player gets Y + 2.0.

In case player identification becomes relevant, you are playing in the position of Player 2 in this game.

response:
Let's break down the problem step by step to find the optimal strategy for Player 2 (you) in this decision-making game.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the actions and payoff structure**

Actions:
- A0 → 2
- A1 → 3
- A2 → 4
- A3 → 5

We’ll denote:
- Your action: X (chosen from {2, 3, 4, 5})
- Opponent’s action: Y (chosen from {2, 3, 4, 5})

Payoffs for **you** (Player 2):

| X vs Y | Your Payoff | Opponent’s Payoff |
|--------|-------------|-------------------|
| X = Y  | X           | Y (=X)            |
| X < Y  | X + 2.0     | X - 2.0           |
| X > Y  | Y - 2.0     | Y + 2.0           |

Note: The roles here are symmetric in structure, but you are Player 2. Since the game is symmetric in rules, but you are the second player, your **goal is to maximize your own expected payoff**, assuming the other player is also rational.

But the game is **not symmetric in player roles** because the payoff functions differ based on whether you are higher or lower than the opponent. However, the rules do not make one player special beyond the fact that you are Player 2 — so if both players act identically, payoff will be symmetric.

---

### **Step 2: Look for Nash Equilibrium**

We need to find a **mixed strategy Nash equilibrium** where neither player can improve their expected payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

Let’s denote:
- You (Player 2): choose a strategy vector $ p = (p_0, p_1, p_2, p_3) $ where $ p_i $ is the probability of choosing action $ A_i $
- Opponent (Player 1): chooses a strategy $ q = (q_0, q_1, q_2, q_3) $

We are to find optimal $ p $, assuming you expect the opponent to play optimally as well.

But here’s a key idea: **since the game is symmetric** (payoff rules apply identically from both sides), the **equilibrium strategies are symmetric**. That is, the equilibrium strategy for both players is the same.

So we can look for a **symmetric mixed strategy Nash equilibrium** where both players use the same mixed strategy $ p $.

---

### **Step 3: Try to find symmetry in payoffs**

Let’s compute the **expected payoff for Player 2** when you choose action X, and opponent plays a symmetric strategy $ p $. Then, in equilibrium, all actions that are played with positive probability should give the same expected payoff.

Let’s suppose both players use the same mixed strategy $ p = (p_0, p_1, p_2, p_3) $, with $ p_0 + p_1 + p_2 + p_3 = 1 $.

Let’s compute the **expected payoff to a player when they play action A_i**, given that the opponent plays strategy $ p $.

We’ll denote $ E(i|p) $ = expected payoff to Player 2 when they play action $ i $ (i.e., value X = value of A_i), and opponent plays $ p $.

We are solving for $ p $ such that all $ i $ with $ p_i > 0 $ yield the same expected payoff, and $ E(i|p) $ is maximized.

---

Let’s define the value of each action:

- A0: X = 2
- A1: X = 3
- A2: X = 4
- A3: X = 5

Let’s compute $ E(i|p) $ for each $ i $, as a function of $ p $.

---

#### **Case: You play A0 (X = 2)**

- If opponent plays Y = 2 (A0): X = Y → you get 2
- If opponent plays Y = 3 (A1): X < Y → you get 2 + 2 = 4
- If opponent plays Y = 4 (A2): X < Y → 4
- If opponent plays Y = 5 (A3): X < Y → 4

So:

$$
E(0|p) = 2 \cdot p_0 + 4 \cdot (p_1 + p_2 + p_3)
$$

$$
E(0|p) = 2p_0 + 4(1 - p_0) = 2p_0 + 4 - 4p_0 = 4 - 2p_0
$$

---

#### **Case: You play A1 (X = 3)**

- Y = 2: X > Y → you get Y - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0
- Y = 3: X = Y → you get 3
- Y = 4: X < Y → you get 3 + 2 = 5
- Y = 5: X < Y → 5

So:

$$
E(1|p) = 0 \cdot p_0 + 3 \cdot p_1 + 5 \cdot (p_2 + p_3)
$$

$$
E(1|p) = 3p_1 + 5(1 - p_0 - p_1) = 3p_1 + 5 - 5p_0 - 5p_1 = 5 - 5p_0 - 2p_1
$$

---

#### **Case: You play A2 (X = 4)**

- Y = 2: X > Y → you get 2 - 2 = 0
- Y = 3: X > Y → you get 3 - 2 = 1
- Y = 4: X = Y → you get 4
- Y = 5: X < Y → you get 4 + 2 = 6

So:

$$
E(2|p) = 0 \cdot p_0 + 1 \cdot p_1 + 4 \cdot p_2 + 6 \cdot p_3
$$

$$
E(2|p) = p_1 + 4p_2 + 6p_3
$$

---

#### **Case: You play A3 (X = 5)**

- Y = 2: X > Y → you get 2 - 2 = 0
- Y = 3: X > Y → 3 - 2 = 1
- Y = 4: X > Y → 4 - 2 = 2
- Y = 5: X = Y → you get 5

So:

$$
E(3|p) = 0 \cdot p_0 + 1 \cdot p_1 + 2 \cdot p_2 + 5 \cdot p_3
$$

$$
E(3|p) = p_1 + 2p_2 + 5p_3
$$

---

### **Step 4: Assume symmetric equilibrium**

In symmetric equilibrium, all actions played with positive probability must have equal expected payoff.

Suppose we try to find a symmetric equilibrium. Let’s **assume all actions are played with positive probability**. Then all four expected values must be equal.

Set:
- $ E(0|p) = E(1|p) = E(2|p) = E(3|p) = v $

So:

1. $ 4 - 2p_0 = v $
2. $ 5 - 5p_0 - 2p_1 = v $
3. $ p_1 + 4p_2 + 6p_3 = v $
4. $ p_1 + 2p_2 + 5p_3 = v $

Also, $ p_0 + p_1 + p_2 + p_3 = 1 $

Let’s solve this system.

From (1): $ v = 4 - 2p_0 $

Plug into (2):

$ 5 - 5p_0 - 2p_1 = 4 - 2p_0 $

→ $ 5 - 5p_0 - 2p_1 = 4 - 2p_0 $

→ $ 1 = 3p_0 + 2p_1 $ → Equation A: $ 3p_0 + 2p_1 = 1 $

Now Equation (3) and (4):

(3): $ p_1 + 4p_2 + 6p_3 = v = 4 - 2p_0 $

(4): $ p_1 + 2p_2 + 5p_3 = 4 - 2p_0 $

Subtract (4) from (3):

→ $ (p_1 + 4p_2 + 6p_3) - (p_1 + 2p_2 + 5p_3) = 0 $

→ $ 2p_2 + p_3 = 0 $

Since probabilities are ≥ 0, this implies:

→ $ 2p_2 + p_3 = 0 $ → $ p_2 = 0, p_3 = 0 $

That’s a contradiction if we assumed all are played with positive probability.

Hence, **not all actions can be played in equilibrium**.

So we can rule out full support.

This means, in equilibrium, **some actions are never played**.

Go back.

We have $ 2p_2 + p_3 = 0 $ from the difference of (3) and (4), which forces $ p_2 = p_3 = 0 $ if they are both played and equal payoff.

But maybe they are not both played. Maybe only some actions are used.

So possibly, **only A0, A1, A2, or A1, A2, A3 are used**.

But note: E(2|p) and E(3|p) both have $ p_1 $, but E(2) has higher coefficients on $ p_2 $ and $ p_3 $ — so likely A2 is better than A3 when opponent plays low.

Try different candidate equilibria.

- A2: correspond to the number 4
- A3: correspond to the number 5

Basic game rules:
1. You and the other player each choose a probability for each action, simultaneously.
2. After both decisions are locked in, the final action will be drawn from the probability distributions.
3. Both players receive the points specified in the payoff description below.

Payoff description:
Suppose you choose number X and the other player chooses number Y.
    - If X = Y: you get X points, the other player gets Y (=X) points.
    - If X < Y: you get X + 2.0, the other player gets X - 2.0.
    - If X > Y: you get Y - 2.0, the other player gets Y + 2.0.

In case player identification becomes relevant, you are playing in the position of Player 2 in this game.

===== Response [ID: 994310d9] [2026-01-19 21:07:52] =====
agent: anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5(CoT)#P2
response:
Looking at this game step by step:

## Step 1: Understanding the Payoff Structure

Let me create my payoff matrix (what I receive for each combination):

```
              Opponent chooses:
              A0(2)  A1(3)  A2(4)  A3(5)
I choose:
A0(2)          2      4      4      4
A1(3)          0      3      5      5
A2(4)          0      1      4      6
A3(5)          0      1      2      5
```

## Step 2: Finding Best Responses

For each opponent action, what's my best response?

- **If opponent plays A0(2)**: A0 gives 2, others give 0 → Best: **A0**
- **If opponent plays A1(3)**: A0 gives 4, A1 gives 3, A2 gives 1, A3 gives 1 → Best: **A0**
- **If opponent plays A2(4)**: A0 gives 4, A1 gives 5, A2 gives 4, A3 gives 2 → Best: **A1**
- **If opponent plays A3(5)**: A0 gives 4, A1 gives 5, A2 gives 6, A3 gives 5 → Best: **A2**

## Step 3: Finding Nash Equilibrium

Since this is a symmetric game, the opponent faces the same payoff structure. Their best responses mirror mine.

The pure strategy Nash equilibrium occurs when both players' strategies are mutual best responses. This happens at **(A0, A0)**:
- If opponent plays A0, my best response is A0
- If I play A0, opponent's best response is A0

This gives both players a payoff of 2.

## Step 4: Checking for Better Equilibria

Could we coordinate on something better?
- (A1, A1): Gives 3 each, but opponent would deviate to A0 (getting 4)
- (A2, A2): Gives 4 each, but opponent would deviate to A1 (getting 5)  
- (A3, A3): Gives 5 each, but opponent would deviate to A2 (getting 6)

None of these are stable equilibria.

## Step 5: Mixed Strategy Consideration

While mixed strategies exist in game theory, the dominant pure strategy here is A0, which:
- Guarantees at least 2 points
- Performs well against most opponent strategies
- Is the unique pure Nash equilibrium

## Conclusion

The optimal strategy is to play **A0** with certainty.

{"A0": 100, "A1": 0, "A2": 0, "A3": 0}

response:
Looking at this game step by step:

## Step 1: Understanding the Payoff Structure

When I choose X and opponent chooses Y:
- X = Y: I get X points
- X < Y: I get X + 2 points  
- X > Y: I get Y - 2 points

## Step 2: Creating My Payoff Matrix

Let me map out my payoffs for all combinations:

```
                Opponent's Choice
                2    3    4    5
My Choice  2:   2    4    4    4
           3:   0    3    5    5
           4:   0    1    4    6
           5:   0    1    2    5
```

## Step 3: Finding Best Responses

If opponent plays:
- **2**: My best response is 2 (getting 2)
- **3**: My best response is 2 (getting 4)
- **4**: My best response is 3 (getting 5)
- **5**: My best response is 4 (getting 6)

## Step 4: Identifying Nash Equilibrium

Due to symmetry, the opponent faces the same payoff structure. 

Checking **(2, 2)** as a pure strategy Nash equilibrium:
- If opponent plays 2, my best response is 2 ✓
- If I play 2, opponent's best response is 2 ✓

This is a Nash equilibrium! Both players get 2 points, and neither player has an incentive to unilaterally deviate (any higher choice against 2 yields 0 points).

## Step 5: Checking for Better Equilibria

While coordinating on higher equal numbers (like 5,5) would give more points, these are not stable Nash equilibria in a simultaneous game - there's always an incentive for one player to undercut by choosing slightly lower to get the bonus.

The **(2, 2)** equilibrium is the unique **risk-dominant** equilibrium that both players can safely coordinate on.

## Conclusion

Playing action A0 (number 2) with 100% probability is the rational choice, as it:
- Forms a Nash equilibrium when opponent does the same
- Protects against exploitation (choosing higher numbers risks getting 0 points)
- Guarantees a positive payoff

===== Response [ID: e05ac289] [2026-01-19 21:08:20] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's solve this step by step.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the Actions and Their Values**

Each action corresponds to a number:
- A0 → 2
- A1 → 3
- A2 → 4
- A3 → 5

So we're choosing numbers from {2, 3, 4, 5}, and the payoff depends on relative comparison.

---

### **Step 2: Analyze the Payoff Rules**

Let X = player's number, Y = opponent's number.

- **If X = Y**: Both get X points.
- **If X < Y**: You get X + 2.0, opponent gets X - 2.0.
- **If X > Y**: You get Y - 2.0, opponent gets Y + 2.0.

💡 **Key Observations**:

- When you **tie**, you get the value of the number (e.g., both get 4 if both choose 4).
- When you **lose (X < Y)**, you get **more than your number** (+2), while opponent gets **less** (-2). This is **good for you** if you’re the one losing.
- When you **win (X > Y)**, you get **less than your number** (-2), while opponent gets more. **Bad for you**.

Wait — this is **counterintuitive**. Let's re-analyze:

- X < Y → You get X + 2, opponent gets X - 2  
  → You gain (relative to your number), opponent loses.
- X > Y → You get Y - 2, opponent gets Y + 2  
  → You **lose** (you get less than your own number), opponent gains.

So:
- **It’s good for you to lose**, because you get +2.
- **It’s bad for you to win**, because you get -2.
- Only **tying** gives you the base value.

This means:
- You benefit from choosing a **lower number** than your opponent.
- You are punished for choosing a **higher number** than your opponent.
- So, **you want to be lower than opponent**, but not too low, because you only get X + 2, not more.

Let’s test with examples:

#### Example 1: You choose A0 (2), Opponent chooses A1 (3)
- X = 2, Y = 3 → X < Y
- You get: 2 + 2 = 4
- Opponent gets: 2 - 2 = 0

You gain 4. Opponent gets 0.

#### Example 2: You choose A1 (3), Opponent chooses A0 (2)
- X = 3, Y = 2 → X > Y
- You get: 2 - 2 = 0
- Opponent gets: 2 + 2 = 4

You get 0. Bad.

#### Example 3: Both choose A1 (3)
- Tie → you get 3

So you’re better off choosing a low number **if you can get your opponent to pick a higher number**.

But your opponent is also rational and will try to maximize their own payoff.

---

### **Step 3: Game Theory – Is This a Zero-Sum Game?**

Let’s check total payoff:

- X = Y: total = X + Y = 2X
- X < Y: you get X+2, opponent gets X-2 → total = (X+2) + (X-2) = 2X
- X > Y: you get Y-2, opponent gets Y+2 → total = (Y-2) + (Y+2) = 2Y

Wait, total is **not constant**.

- X = Y → total = 2X
- X < Y → total = 2X
- X > Y → total = 2Y

So total depends on outcome.

But importantly: **if X < Y**, total = 2X  
if X > Y, total = 2Y  
if X = Y, total = 2X = 2Y

So the total points depend on the **lower** of the two numbers in all cases?

Wait:
- When X < Y → total = 2X (X is lower)
- When X > Y → total = 2Y (Y is lower)
- When X = Y → total = 2X = 2Y

✅ So **in all cases, the total points awarded is twice the lower of the two numbers**.

That’s a key insight.

So **total payoff = 2 × min(X, Y)**

And the split:
- If X < Y: you get X+2, opponent X-2 → you get more than X, opponent less
- If X > Y: you get Y-2, opponent Y+2 → you get less, opponent more
- If X = Y: both get X

But **the total remains 2 × min(X,Y)**.

Now, since the total is fixed for a given pair, and we are **Player 1**, we want to **maximize our own payoff**, not total.

---

### **Step 4: Strategy Implications**

Let’s analyze how your payoff depends on your choice and opponent's.

Let’s define your payoff for various (X, Y):

| X \ Y | 2 (A0) | 3 (A1) | 4 (A2) | 5 (A3) |
|-------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| 2 (A0) | 2 | 2+2=4 | 2+2=4 | 2+2=4 |
| 3 (A1) | 3-2=1 | 3 | 3-2=1 | 3-2=1 |
| 4 (A2) | 4-2=2 | 4-2=2 | 4 | 4-2=2 |
| 5 (A3) | 5-2=3 | 5-2=3 | 5-2=3 | 5 |

Let’s fill this table carefully.

#### X = 2 (A0):
- Y = 2 → tie → you get 2
- Y = 3 → X < Y → you get 2 + 2 = 4
- Y = 4 → X < Y → 2 + 2 = 4
- Y = 5 → X < Y → 2 + 2 = 4

→ So for A0, you get: 2, 4, 4, 4

#### X = 3 (A1):
- Y = 2 → X > Y → you get Y - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0
- Y = 3 → tie → 3
- Y = 4 → X < Y → 3 + 2 = 5
- Y = 5 → X < Y → 3 + 2 = 5

→ But wait: earlier I said if X < Y → you get X + 2

Yes, so:
- X = 3, Y = 4 → X < Y → +2 → 5
- X = 3, Y = 5 → 5

But if X > Y: you get Y - 2

So:
- X = 3, Y = 2 → 2 - 2 = 0

→ So for A1: 0, 3, 5, 5

#### X = 4 (A2):
- Y = 2 → X > Y → you get 2 - 2 = 0
- Y = 3 → X > Y → 3 - 2 = 1
- Y = 4 → tie → 4
- Y = 5 → X < Y → 4 + 2 = 6

→ So: 0, 1, 4, 6

#### X = 5 (A3):
- Y = 2 → X > Y → 2 - 2 = 0
- Y = 3 → X > Y → 3 - 2 = 1
- Y = 4 → X > Y → 4 - 2 = 2
- Y = 5 → tie → 5

→ So: 0, 1, 2, 5

Now, let's write the full payoff matrix (Player 1's payoff):

|          | A0 (2) | A1 (3) | A2 (4) | A3 (5) |
|----------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| **A0**   | 2      | 4      | 4      | 4      |
| **A1**   | 0      | 3      | 5      | 5      |
| **A2**   | 0      | 1      | 4      | 6      |
| **A3**   | 0      | 1      | 2      | 5      |

### **Step 5: Find the Optimal Strategy**

This is a **symmetric zero-sum-like** game, but not zero-sum (total varies), but we can still look for **Nash equilibrium** in mixed strategies.

We want to find a **mixed strategy** (probability distribution) over actions that **maximizes your minimum expected payoff**, assuming the opponent is also rational and will play optimally.

But since the game is symmetric (same payoff structure for both players), **there is likely a symmetric Nash equilibrium**.

Let’s try to find it.

Let’s suppose the opponent plays a mixed strategy:
- p0 = P(A0)
- p1 = P(A1)
- p2 = P(A2)
- p3 = P(A3)

Your expected payoff if you play:
- A0: 2*p0 + 4*p1 + 4*p2 + 4*p3 = 2p0 + 4(1 - p0) = 2p0 + 4 - 4p0 = 4 - 2p0
- A1: 0*p0 + 3*p1 + 5*p2 + 5*p3 = 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3
- A2: 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3
- A3: 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 2*p2 + 5*p3 = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

But in a symmetric equilibrium, both players play the same strategy.

So suppose both players play (p0, p1, p2, p3). Then, the expected payoff for each action must be **equal** (otherwise, you’d deviate to a better one). That’s the key to symmetric equilibrium.

So we set:
- E(A0) = E(A1) = E(A2) = E(A3)

Let’s denote:
- E0 = 4 - 2p0
- E1 = 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3
- E2 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3
- E3 = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

Also, p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1

Let’s try to find such p0, p1, p2, p3.

Let’s assume **p0 > 0**, but maybe small. Also, A0 gives 4 when opponent plays 3 or 4 or 5 — but only 2 if opponent plays A0.

So A0 is strong against high plays.

But A0 gives 4 only when opponent plays above 2 — which is bad for opponent.

But if opponent plays A0 often, then you should avoid A0.

Let’s try to assume the equilibrium has **no A3** (A3 gives 0 when opponent plays A0, and only 5 if opponent plays A3 — risky). A3 is dangerous.

Similarly, A (A1) only gives 0 if opponent plays A0.

Try **p3 = 0**

Then p0 + p1 + p2 = 1

- E1 = 3p1 + 5p2
- E2 = p1 + 4p2
- E3 = p1 + 2p2 (but p3=0, so we don’t use it)

### **Step 2: Analyze the payoffs for different comparisons**

---

### **Step 1: Understand the actions and their values**
Actions:
- A0 → 2
- A1 → 3
- A2 → 4
- A3 → 5

So the possible numbers chosen are 2, 3, 4, 5.

---

### **Step 2: Understand the payoff rules**
Let:
- X = your number (Player 1)
- Y = opponent's number (Player 2)

Payoffs to **Player 1**:
- If X = Y: you get **X** points
- If X < Y: you get **X + 2.0**
- If X > Y: you get **Y - 2.0**

Similarly, for Player 2:
- If X = Y: Y points
- If X < Y: X - 2.0
- If X > Y: Y + 2.0

We are only concerned with maximizing **our own** total expected points.

---

### **Step 3: Analyze the payoffs for Player 1 in each case**

We can make a payoff table for Player 1, with rows = your action (X), columns = opponent’s action (Y), and cell = your payoff.

| X\Y | 2 (A0) | 3 (A1) | 4 (A2) | 5 (A3) |
|-----|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| 2 (A0) | 2      | 2+2=4  | 2+2=4  | 2+2=4  |

| 5 (A3) | 5-2=3  | 5-2=3  | 5-2=3  | 5      |

- If X = Y → X points.

- If X > Y → Y - 2

This is a **zero-sum-like** game? No, not exactly — payoffs are not additive negatives.

Actually, no: the payoffs are **not symmetric**. For example:

- Opponent picks 2, you pick 5: X > Y → you get 2 - 2 = 0

So getting to pick a low number when opponent picks high is good; getting to pick high when opponent picks low is **very bad**.

### **Step 5: Consider Nash Equilibrium**

We assume both players are rational and will try to maximize their expected payoff. We are to find a strategy (a probability distribution over A0–A3) that is **in equilibrium** — i.e., a best response to itself.

Let’s suppose both players are symmetric in their strategy space, and we look for a symmetric Nash equilibrium.

Let’s try to find if there is a mixed strategy where no player can gain by deviating.

Let the strategy be:
- p0 = P(A0) = P(X=2)
- p1 = P(A1) = P(X=3)
- p2 = P(A2) = P(X=4)
- p3 = P(A3) = P(X=5)

With p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 100%

We want to choose this distribution so that **all actions available (with positive probability) have the same expected payoff**, and all other actions (with zero probability) have lower or equal expected payoff.

This is the **indifference condition** in mixed strategy Nash equilibrium.

But this can be complex. Instead, let’s analyze **expected payoff** of each action against a given distribution.

You are playing a decision-making game with another player.

    - If X > Y: you get Y - 2.0, the other player gets Y + 2.0.

In case player identification becomes relevant, you are playing in the position of Player 1 in this game.

- Choose a probability distribution over ALL actions each round.
- Output must contain a valid JSON object at the end.

response:
Let's break this down step by step to find the optimal strategy for Player 1 in this symmetric, zero-sum-like game.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Payoff Structure

Each player chooses an action:
- A0 → 2
- A1 → 3
- A2 → 4
- A3 → 5

Let X be Player 1’s number, Y be Player 2’s number.

Payoffs:
- If X = Y:  
  - Player 1 gets X points  
  - Player 2 gets Y = X points  
- If X < Y:  
  - Player 1 gets X + 2.0  
  - Player 2 gets X - 2.0  
- If X > Y:  
  - Player 1 gets Y - 2.0  
  - Player 2 gets Y + 2.0

Note: This game is **not symmetric** in payoffs. Even though both players are subject to the same rules, the payoff differs depending on who has the higher number. Importantly, if X > Y, then Player 1 gets **Y - 2.0**, which can be negative (bad), while Player 2 gets **Y + 2.0** (good). So trying to make your number larger can backfire if the opponent chooses a lower number.

---

### Step 2: Analyze the Implications

Let’s examine how each outcome affects Player 1 (you).

#### Case 1: You choose X, other player chooses Y

| X \ Y | A0 (2) | A1 (3) | A2 (4) | A3 (5) |
|-------|--------|--------|--------|--------|

| A2 (4) | 4-2=2 | 4-2=2 | 4     | 4+2=6  |

- (4,4): 4
- (5,5): 5

### Step 3: Can We Find a Nash Equilibrium?

We are to find a mixed strategy (probability distribution over actions) for Player 1 that is **best response** to itself, or at least robust to any strategy Player 2 might use — ideally, a symmetric Nash equilibrium.

Since the game is symmetric in structure (same rules for both players), we can look for a symmetric Nash equilibrium where both players use the same mixed strategy.

Let the strategy be:
- p0 = P(A0) = P(X=2)
- p1 = P(A1) = P(X=3)
- p2 = P(A2) = P(X=4)
- p3 = P(A3) = P(X=5)

We seek p0, p1, p2, p3 ≥ 0, sum to 100 (or 1 in probability), such that no player can gain by deviating.

In symmetric equilibrium, the expected payoff of each pure strategy (when the opponent uses the equilibrium strategy) should be equal (if the strategy is mixed), or higher for the ones played with positive probability.

So let’s compute the **expected payoff for Player 1** when choosing each action, given that Player 2 uses strategy (p0, p1, p2, p3).

Let’s compute Expected Payoff (EP) for each action:

#### EP(A0) = payoff when you play A0 (X=2), opponent plays (p0, p1, p2, p3)

- If opponent plays A0 (Y=2): X=Y → payoff = 2 → contribution: p0 * 2
- If opponent A1 (Y=3): X=2 < Y=3 → payoff = 2+2 = 4 → p1 * 4
- If opponent A2 (Y=4): X < Y → 4 → p2 * 4
- If opponent A3 (Y=5): X < Y → 4 → p3 * 4

So:
> EP(A0) = 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 + 4p3

But note: p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1 → p1 + p2 + p3 = 1 - p0

So:
EP(A0) = 2p0 + 4(1 - p0) = 2p0 + 4 - 4p0 = 4 - 2p0

#### EP(A1) = when you play X=3

- Y=2 (A0): X > Y → payoff = Y - 2 = 0 → p0 * 0 = 0
- Y=3: X=Y → payoff = 3 → p1 * 3
- Y=4: X < Y → payoff = 3 + 2 = 5 → p2 * 5
- Y=5: X < Y → 5 → p3 * 5

So:
EP(A1) = 0*p0 + 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3 = 3p1 + 5(p2 + p3)

Since p2 + p3 = 1 - p0 - p1:
= 3p1 + 5(1 - p0 - p1) = 3p1 + 5 - 5p0 - 5p1 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1

#### EP(A2) = X=4

- Y=2: X > Y → payoff = 2 - 2 = 0 → p0*0
- Y=3: X > Y → payoff = 3 - 2 = 1 → p1*1
- Y=4: X=Y → 4 → p2*4
- Y=5: X < Y → 4+2=6 → p3*6

So:
EP(A2) = 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3

#### EP(A3) = X=5

- Y=2: X > Y → 2-2 = 0 → p0*0
- Y=3: X > Y → 3-2 = 1 → p1*1
- Y=4: X > Y → 4-2 = 2 → p2*2
- Y=5: X=Y → 5 → p3*5

So:
EP(A3) = 0 + 1*p1 + 2*p2 + 5*p3 = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

---

### Step 4: Assume Symmetric Equilibrium

In a symmetric Nash equilibrium, all actions played with positive probability must have **equal expected payoff**. If one has higher payoff, we’d switch to it.

Let’s suppose all four actions are used with positive probability. Then:

> EP(A0) = EP(A1) = EP(A2) = EP(A3)

Let’s denote this common value as E.

So:

1. EP(A0) = 4 - 2p0 = E  
2. EP(A1) = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1 = E  
3. EP(A2) = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 = E  
4. EP(A3) = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3 = E  

And p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1

Let’s try to solve this system.

---

From (1):  
E = 4 - 2p0 → p0 = (4 - E)/2

Now plug into (2):

5 - 5p0 - 2p1 = E  
→ 5 - 5*(4 - E)/2 - 2p1 = E  
Multiply through by 2 to eliminate denominators:

10 - 5(4 - E) - 4p1 = 2E  
→ 10 - 20 + 5E - 4p1 = 2E  
→ -10 + 5E - 4p1 = 2E  
→ 3E - 4p1 = 10  
→ 4p1 = 3E - 10  
→ p1 = (3E - 10)/4

Now use equation (4): EP(A3) = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3 = E

We’ll hold onto that.

Use equation (3): p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 = E

Now take (3) - (4):

(p1 + 4p2 + 6p3) - (p1 + 2p2 + 5p3) = E - E  
→ 2p2 + p3 = 0

But p2, p3 ≥ 0 → 2p2 + p3 = 0 → p2 = 0, p3 = 0

This is key!

So from (3)-(4), we get:
2p2 + p3 = 0 → p2 = 0, p3 = 0

But then all mass is on A0 and A1.

Now recall: p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1 → p0 + p1 = 1

Now go back:

p2 = 0, p3 = 0 → from above.

Now check equation (3):

EP(A2) = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 = p1 = E

But from earlier:
E = 4 - 2p0  
And p0 = 1 - p1

So:
E = 4 - 2(1 - p1) = 4 - 2 + 2p1 = 2 + 2p1

But EP(A2) = p1 → set equal:

p1 = E = 2 + 2p1 → p1 = 2 + 2p1 → -p1 = 2 → p1 = -2 → invalid

Contradiction.

So our assumption that all four actions are used in equilibrium must be wrong.

Thus, **not all actions are played with positive probability**.

So some actions have negative expected payoff, so player won’t play them.

But A3 (5) is tempting: if you play 5 and opponent plays 2,3,4, you get 0,1,2 — small, but if opponent plays 5, you get 5. But if opponent plays 2-4, you risk "over-investing" and get low payoff (0,1,2) while opponent gets high (2+2=4, 3+2=5, 4+2=6).

So A3 is risky.

Earlier we found that if all are played, contradiction occurs.

Now try **assume only A0, A1, A2** are played (p3 = 0)

Try to find equilibrium with p3 = 0, p2 > 0

But from earlier, (3)-(4) gives 2p2 + p3 = 0 → even if p3=0, then 2p2 = 0 → p2=0 → contradiction.

Wait — no: (3)-(4) = 2p2 + p3 = 0 — this came from **assuming that EP(A2) = EP(A3)**, which **only holds if both actions are played**.

But if **A3 is not played**, we do **not** require EP(A3) = EP(A2); we just require that EP(A3) ≤ EP(A2), and similarly for other actions.

So perhaps A3 is not played.

Let’s try setting p3 = 0, and see if we can find a symmetric equilibrium with p2, p1, p0.

So possible actions: A0, A1, A2 (values 2,3,4)

Now p3 = 0, p0 + p1 + p2 = 1

Now compute expected payoffs:

#### EP(A0) = 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 = 2p0 + 4(1 - p0) = 4 - 2p0

#### EP(A1) = 0*p0 + 3p1 + 5p2 = 3p1 + 5p2

### Step 3: Can We Find a Nash Equilibrium?

We are to find a mixed strategy (probability distribution over actions) for Player 1 that is **best response** to itself, or at least robust to any strategy Player 2 might use — ideally, a symmetric Nash equilibrium.

Since the game is symmetric in structure (same rules for both players), we can look for a symmetric Nash equilibrium where both players use the same mixed strategy.

Let the strategy be:
- p0 = P(A0) = P(X=2)
- p1 = P(A1) = P(X=3)
- p2 = P(A2) = P(X=4)
- p3 = P(A3) = P(X=5)

We seek p0, p1, p2, p3 ≥ 0, sum to 100 (or 1 in probability), such that no player can gain by deviating.

In symmetric equilibrium, the expected payoff of each pure strategy (when the opponent uses the equilibrium strategy) should be equal (if the strategy is mixed), or higher for the ones played with positive probability.

So let’s compute the **expected payoff for Player 1** when choosing each action, given that Player 2 uses strategy (p0, p1, p2, p3).

Let’s compute Expected Payoff (EP) for each action:

#### EP(A0) = payoff when you play A0 (X=2), opponent plays (p0, p1, p2, p3)

- If opponent plays A0 (Y=2): X=Y → payoff = 2 → contribution: p0 * 2
- If opponent A1 (Y=3): X=2 < Y=3 → payoff = 2+2 = 4 → p1 * 4
- If opponent A2 (Y=4): X < Y → 4 → p2 * 4
- If opponent A3 (Y=5): X < Y → 4 → p3 * 4

So:
> EP(A0) = 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 + 4p3

But note: p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1 → p1 + p2 + p3 = 1 - p0

So:
EP(A0) = 2p0 + 4(1 - p0) = 2p0 + 4 - 4p0 = 4 - 2p0

#### EP(A1) = when you play X=3

- Y=2 (A0): X > Y → payoff = Y - 2 = 0 → p0 * 0 = 0
- Y=3: X=Y → payoff = 3 → p1 * 3
- Y=4: X < Y → payoff = 3 + 2 = 5 → p2 * 5
- Y=5: X < Y → 5 → p3 * 5

So:
EP(A1) = 0*p0 + 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3 = 3p1 + 5(p2 + p3)

Since p2 + p3 = 1 - p0 - p1:
= 3p1 + 5(1 - p0 - p1) = 3p1 + 5 - 5p0 - 5p1 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1

#### EP(A2) = X=4

- Y=2: X > Y → payoff = 2 - 2 = 0 → p0*0
- Y=3: X > Y → payoff = 3 - 2 = 1 → p1*1
- Y=4: X=Y → 4 → p2*4
- Y=5: X < Y → 4+2=6 → p3*6

So:
EP(A2) = 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3 = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3

#### EP(A3) = X=5

- Y=2: X > Y → 2-2 = 0 → p0*0
- Y=3: X > Y → 3-2 = 1 → p1*1
- Y=4: X > Y → 4-2 = 2 → p2*2
- Y=5: X=Y → 5 → p3*5

So:
EP(A3) = 0 + 1*p1 + 2*p2 + 5*p3 = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

---

### Step 4: Assume Symmetric Equilibrium

In a symmetric Nash equilibrium, all actions played with positive probability must have **equal expected payoff**. If one has higher payoff, we’d switch to it.

Let’s suppose all four actions are used with positive probability. Then:

> EP(A0) = EP(A1) = EP(A2) = EP(A3)

Let’s denote this common value as E.

So:

1. EP(A0) = 4 - 2p0 = E  
2. EP(A1) = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1 = E  
3. EP(A2) = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 = E  
4. EP(A3) = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3 = E  

And p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1

Let’s try to solve this system.

---

From (1):  
E = 4 - 2p0 → p0 = (4 - E)/2

Now plug into (2):

5 - 5p0 - 2p1 = E  
→ 5 - 5*(4 - E)/2 - 2p1 = E  
Multiply through by 2 to eliminate denominators:

10 - 5(4 - E) - 4p1 = 2E  
→ 10 - 20 + 5E - 4p1 = 2E  
→ -10 + 5E - 4p1 = 2E  
→ 3E - 4p1 = 10  
→ 4p1 = 3E - 10  
→ p1 = (3E - 10)/4

Now use equation (4): EP(A3) = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3 = E

We’ll hold onto that.

Use equation (3): p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 = E

Now take (3) - (4):

(p1 + 4p2 + 6p3) - (p1 + 2p2 + 5p3) = E - E  
→ 2p2 + p3 = 0

But p2, p3 ≥ 0 → 2p2 + p3 = 0 → p2 = 0, p3 = 0

This is key!

So from (3)-(4), we get:
2p2 + p3 = 0 → p2 = 0, p3 = 0

But then all mass is on A0 and A1.

Now recall: p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1 → p0 + p1 = 1

Now go back:

p2 = 0, p3 = 0 → from above.

Now check equation (3):

EP(A2) = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3 = p1 = E

But from earlier:
E = 4 - 2p0  
And p0 = 1 - p1

So:
E = 4 - 2(1 - p1) = 4 - 2 + 2p1 = 2 + 2p1

But EP(A2) = p1 → set equal:

p1 = E = 2 + 2p1 → p1 = 2 + 2p1 → -p1 = 2 → p1 = -2 → invalid

Contradiction.

So our assumption that all four actions are used in equilibrium must be wrong.

- If p2 > 0, then EP(A2) ≥ EP(A0), EP(A2) ≥ EP(A1)

Let’s carefully re-analyze the game to determine the optimal strategy for Player 1.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Payoff Matrix (Player 1's perspective)

We define the payoff for Player 1 based on their choice X and opponent's choice Y:

| X \ Y | A0 (2) | A1 (3) | A2 (4) | A3 (5) |
|-------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| A0 (2) | 2     | 4     | 4     | 4     |
| A1 (3) | 0     | 3     | 5     | 5     |
| A2 (4) | 0     | 1     | 4     | 6     |
| A3 (5) | 0     | 1     | 2     | 5     |

This matrix shows Player 1’s earnings.

Key observations:

- **A0 (2)** is safe: always gives at least 2, and up to 4 if opponent plays higher. But if you play higher than them, you risk getting low.
- **A1 (3)** can give 0 if opponent plays 2 — very bad. But if they play 4 or 5, you get 5.
- **A2 (4)**: If opponent plays 2 or 3, you get 0 or 1 — low. But if opponent plays 5, you get 6.
- **A3 (5)**: You get 0 if opponent plays 2 (since Y=2 → Y-2=0), 1 if Y=3, 2 if Y=4, and 5 if Y=5.

But note: **When you choose a higher number than the opponent (X > Y), you get only Y - 2**, which means you're penalized for overshooting.

Thus, playing high numbers like A2 or A3 is risky if the opponent plays low.

---

### Step 2: Is There a Symmetric Nash Equilibrium?

We test for symmetric mixed-strategy equilibrium: both players use same strategy.

Try to find a strategy where all actions have equal expected payoff.

Let’s suppose a symmetric equilibrium exists where all four actions are played with positive probability.

From earlier derivation:

- EP(A0) = 4 - 2p0
- EP(A1) = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1
- EP(A2) = p1 + 4p2 + 6p3
- EP(A3) = p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

Set EP(A2) = EP(A3):
→ (p1 + 4p2 + 6p3) = (p1 + 2p2 + 5p3)
→ 2p2 + p3 = 0 → p2 = p3 = 0

So **cannot play both A2 and A3** if they are equally valuable.

Hence, any symmetric equilibrium must exclude one of A2 or A3.

Now try **setting p3 = 0** → do not play A3.

Then focus on A0, A1, A2.

- p0 = P(A0)
- p1 = P(A1)
- p2 = P(A2)
- p0 + p1 + p2 = 1

Expected payoffs:
- EP(A0) = 4 - 2p0
- EP(A1) = 3p1 + 5p2
- EP(A2) = p1 + 4p2

Suppose all three are played. Then:
EP(A0) = EP(A1) = EP(A2) = E

So:
1. E = 4 - 2p0 → p0 = (4 - E)/2  
2. E = 3p1 + 5p2  
3. E = p1 + 4p2  

(2)-(3): (3p1 + 5p2) - (p1 + 4p2) = 0 → 2p1 + p2 = 0 → p1 = p2 = 0 → contradiction since then p0 = 1.

So cannot have all three played.

Try only **A0 and A1** (p2 = p3 = 0)

Then:
- EP(A0) = 4 - 2p0 = 4 - 2p (if p0 = p)
- EP(A1) = 3(1-p) = 3 - 3p

Set equal:  
4 - 2p = 3 - 3p → 1 + p = 0 → p = -1 → impossible.

So no mixed equilibrium between A0 and A1.

Try only A0 and A2 (p1=p3=0)

Then p0 + p2 = 1

EP(A0) = 4 - 2p0  
EP(A2) = 0*p0 + 1*0 + 4*p2 = 4p2 = 4(1 - p0)

So only A2 is played.

Try only A1 and A2.

Let p1 = q, p2 = 1-q, p0=p3=0

EP(A1) = 3q + 5(1−q) = 3q + 5 − 5q = 5 − 2q  
EP(A2) = 1*q + 4*(1−q) = q + 4 − 4q = 4 − 3q

Set equal: 5 − 2q = 4 − 3q → q = -1 → impossible.

No mixed equilibrium.

Now consider that in **all attempts**, the only candidate that survives as a Nash equilibrium is when **both players play A0 (2) exclusively**.

Let’s verify:

- If both play A0: X=Y=2 → both get 2 points.
- If you deviate:
  - Play A1 (3): X=3 > Y=2 → you get Y-2 = 0 < 2 → worse
  - Play A2 (4): Y-2 = 0 < 2 → worse
  - Play A3 (5): Y-2 = 0 < 2 → worse

So no incentive to deviate.

✅ Therefore, **(A0, A0) is a pure Nash equilibrium**.

Is there any other equilibrium?

What if both play A1?  
→ X=Y=3 → both get 3.

Deviation to A0: X=2 < Y=3 → you get 2+2 = 4 > 3 → better!

So A0 is better → you will deviate.

Thus, A1 alone is not stable.

A2 alone: both play 4 → get 4  
Deviating to A1 (3): 3 < 4 → get 3+2 = 5 > 4 → better → not stable.

A3 alone: X=Y=5 → both get 5  
Deviating to A1 (3): X=3 < Y=5 → get 3+2=5 → same  
To A2 (4): 4 < 5 → 4+2=6 > 5 → better → so not stable.

Only A0 has no better deviation.

Moreover, A0 is safer: never gives less than 2, and avoids negative incentives from over-investment.

Even if the opponent plays higher, you gain (X+2), but when you play higher than them, you lose big.

Thus, **playing A0 dominates the risk of going higher**.

Therefore, the **only robust, self-sustaining strategy is to always play A0 (2)**.

---

### Final Decision

To maximize your expected points in this game, and given that the only Nash equilibrium is (A0, A0), and deviations are punished, you should **play A0 with 100% probability**.

Even if it seems suboptimal in some scenarios, in equilibrium, it is the only rational choice.

---

### Output Format

Return a JSON object with integer percentages summing to 100.

```json
{"A0": 100, "A1": 0, "A2": 0, "A3": 0}
```

Actions:

- A1 → 3
- A2 → 4

- You are **penalized when you win (X > Y)**: you get Y - 2 (which can be 0 or negative)
- You get fair payoff when you tie

This creates a **strong incentive to avoid winning**, and a **strong incentive to lose** (by choosing a lower number than the opponent), because losing gives a bonus.

But if you lose by a wide margin (e.g., choosing 2 vs 5), you get 2 + 2 = 4 — still good.

But if you win and opponent chooses a low number (e.g., you choose 5, opponent chooses 2), you get 2 - 2 = 0 → bad.

Thus:
- **High numbers are dangerous** (you risk getting penalized when you win)
- **Low numbers are safer and potentially beneficial** (you gain when you lose)

But the opponent will anticipate this — if you play low, they might **choose low too** to avoid losing points (since they get X - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0 if you play 2 and they play 3, etc.)

So both players are in a game of **strategic understatement** — you both want to pick low numbers to avoid loss, but also to be "low enough" to lose intentionally.

Let:
- p0 = P(A0) = P(X=2)
- p1 = P(A1) = P(X=3)
- p2 = P(A2) = P(X=4)
- p3 = P(A3) = P(X=5)

With p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1

We want to find probabilities such that **no player can gain by deviating**.

So we compute the **expected payoff for each pure strategy** against the opponent’s mixed strategy.

Let’s compute Player 1’s expected payoff for choosing each action, given that Player 2 uses (p0, p1, p2, p3).

---

### **Step 5: Expected payoff for each action (against P2’s strategy)**

Let E(i) = Expected payoff for Player 1 choosing action A_i.

We compute for i=0 to 3.

#### **E(0): Choose X=2**

Payoff depends on Y:
- Y=2 (prob p0): X=Y → payoff = 2
- Y=3 (p1): X<Y → payoff = 2+2 = 4
- Y=4 (p2): X<Y → 4
- Y=5 (p3): X<Y → 4

So E(0) = 2*p0 + 4*p1 + 4*p2 + 4*p3  
= 2*p0 + 4*(1 - p0)  
= 2*p0 + 4 - 4*p0  
= 4 - 2*p0

#### **E(1): Choose X=3**

- Y=2 (p0): X>Y → payoff = Y - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0
- Y=3 (p1): X=Y → 3
- Y=4 (p2): X<Y → 3+2 = 5
- Y=5 (p3): X<Y → 5

So E(1) = 0*p0 + 3*p1 + 5*p2 + 5*p3  
= 3*p1 + 5*(p2 + p3)  
= 3*p1 + 5*(1 - p0 - p1)  
= 3*p1 + 5 - 5*p0 - 5*p1  
= 5 - 5*p0 - 2*p1

#### **E(2): Choose X=4**

- Y=2 (p0): X>Y → Y - 2 = 0
- Y=3 (p1): X>Y → Y - 2 = 1
- Y=4 (p2): X=Y → 4
- Y=5 (p3): X<Y → 4+2 = 6

E(2) = 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3  
= p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3

#### **E(3): Choose X=5**

- Y=2 (p0): X>Y → 0
- Y=3 (p1): X>Y → 1
- Y=4 (p2): X>Y → 2
- Y=5 (p3): X=Y → 5

E(3) = 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 2*p2 + 5*p3  
= p1 + 2*p2 + 5*p3

---

### **Step 6: Find symmetric NE — all actions played with positive probability must have equal EU**

In symmetric Nash equilibrium, if all actions are played with positive probability, then all expected payoffs must be equal.

So suppose p0 > 0, p1 > 0, p2 > 0, p3 > 0 → then:

E(0) = E(1) = E(2) = E(3)

Let’s set:
- E(0) = E(1)
- E(1) = E(2)
- E(2) = E(3)

We’ll solve this system.

Start with **E(0) = E(1)**:

From above:
E(0) = 4 - 2*p0  
E(1) = 5 - 5*p0 - 2*p1

So:
4 - 2*p0 = 5 - 5*p0 - 2*p1  
Bring all to one side:
4 - 2*p0 - 5 + 5*p0 + 2*p1 = 0  
→ 3*p0 + 2*p1 = 1  → Equation (1)

Now **E(1) = E(2)**:

E(1) = 5 - 5*p0 - 2*p1  
E(2) = p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3

But since p3 = 1 - p0 - p1 - p2, substitute:

E(2) = p1 + 4*p2 + 6*(1 - p0 - p1 - p2)  
= p1 + 4*p2 + 6 - 6*p0 - 6*p1 - 6*p2  
= (p1 - 6*p1) + (4*p2 - 6*p2) + 6 - 6*p0  
= -5*p1 - 2*p2 + 6 - 6*p0

Now set E(1) = E(2):

5 - 5*p0 - 2*p1 = -5*p1 - 2*p2 + 6 - 6*p0

Bring all to left:

5 - 5*p0 - 2*p1 + 5*p1 + 2*p2 - 6 + 6*p0 = 0  
→ (5 - 6) + (-5*p0 + 6*p0) + (-2*p1 + 5*p1) + 2*p2 = 0  
→ -1 + p0 + 3*p1 + 2*p2 = 0  
→ p0 + 3*p1 + 2*p2 = 1 → Equation (2)

Now **E(2) = E(3)**:

E(2) = p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3  
E(3) = p1 + 2*p2 + 5*p3

Set equal:

p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3 = p1 + 2*p2 + 5*p3  
Subtract p1 from both:

4*p2 + 6*p3 = 2*p2 + 5*p3  
→ 2*p2 + p3 = 0

But p2 ≥ 0, p3 ≥ 0 → only solution: p2 = 0, p3 = 0

That would imply p2 = p3 = 0

But if p2 = p3 = 0, then p0 + p1 = 1

Wait — is that consistent?

From E(2) = E(3) ⇒ 2*p2 + p3 = 0 ⇒ p2 = p3 = 0

That means **in symmetric equilibrium with all actions active, this is impossible** unless p2=p3=0.

But p2=p3=0 implies we only play A0 and A1.

Let’s test whether **p2=p3=0** can be consistent.

Suppose p2 = p3 = 0 → then p0 + p1 = 1

Now compute E(0), E(1), E(2), E(3)

E(0) = 4 - 2*p0  
E(1) = 5 - 5*p0 - 2*p1 = 5 - 5*p0 - 2*(1 - p0) = 5 - 5*p0 - 2 + 2*p0 = 3 - 3*p0

E(2) = p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3 = p1 = 1 - p0  
E(3) = p1 + 2*p2 + 5*p3 = p1 = 1 - p0

Now, for symmetric equilibrium, we need:
- E(0) = E(1) = E(2) = E(3)

But E(2) = E(3) = 1 - p0

E(0) = 4 - 2*p0  
E(1) = 3 - 3*p0

Set E(0) = E(1):  
4 - 2*p0 = 3 - 3*p0 → p0 = -1 → impossible

So contradiction.

Thus, **no symmetric equilibrium where all four actions are used**.

Alternative idea: perhaps only some actions are used.

Maybe the true equilibrium uses only A1, A2, A3? Or A0, A1, A2?

Let’s instead consider: **would playing A0 (2) ever be optimal?**

From E(0) = 4 - 2*p0

If p0 is high, E(0) is low.

But E(0) = 4 if opponent plays 3,4,5 — which are more than 2 → so you get +2 extra.

But if opponent plays 2, you get only 2.

So it's risky.

But now, consider that **if you play 2, you can get 4 easily when opponent plays higher** — so it’s good **if you think opponent will play high**.

Similarly, **playing 5 is risky**, because if opponent plays 2,3,4 → you get 0,1,2 — low.

Only when opponent plays 5 do you get 5.

So 5 is high-risk, high-reward only when both play 5.

But **if opponent plays 5 → you get 5 if you play 5, but only 2 if you play 4** (since X>Y → Y-2 = 3-2 = 1? Wait no)

Wait: if you play 4 and opponent plays 5 → X=4, Y=5 → X<Y → you get X+2 = 6

Ah! That's better.

So **if you play 4 and opponent plays 5**, you get **6**, which is better than getting 5 when both play 5.

So even better: **playing 4 gives 6 when opponent plays 5**.

Similarly, playing 3 vs 5: you get 5 (X<Y → X+2=5)

Playing 2 vs 5: gets 4

So **higher numbers get more when you underplay**.

But if you **overplay** (e.g., play 5 when opponent plays 4), you get **Y-2 = 2** → bad.

So **preference: you want to be just below the opponent's move**.

This suggests a **"just below" strategy** — like in penalty kicks.

Let’s test what mixed strategy makes **opponent indifferent**.

Assume **we are Player 1**, and we want to maximize our expected payoff.

Let’s suppose **Player 2 is also rational**, and will play a best response to us.

We aim to find a **maximin strategy** — one that maximizes the minimum payoff, or in this case, in equilibrium, a strategy where we can't be exploited.

### **Step 1: Understand the game structure**

We are in a simultaneous two-player game where:

- Each player chooses a move: A0 (2), A1 (3), A2 (4), A3 (5)
- Each player selects a **probability distribution** over these four actions (i.e., mixed strategy)
- The moves are selected independently based on these distributions
- After both moves are chosen, the payoff is determined by the rules:

#### **Payoff Rules (Player 1 and Player 2):**
Let:
- Player 1 chooses number X
- Player 2 chooses number Y

Then:
- If **X = Y**:  
  - Player 1 gets X points  
  - Player 2 gets Y (=X) points

- If **X < Y**:  
  - Player 1 gets X + 2.0  
  - Player 2 gets X - 2.0

- If **X > Y**:  
  - Player 1 gets Y - 2.0  
  - Player 2 gets Y + 2.0

Note: Player 1 is the one we're optimizing for.

---

### **Step 2: Analyze the incentives and strategic behavior**

This is a **non-zero-sum game** with asymmetric payoffs depending on relative size of choices.

Let’s first compute the payoffs for all combinations (X, Y) to understand the game better.

We’ll treat the actions as their numerical values: 2, 3, 4, 5.

| X (P1) | Y (P2) | X=Y? | P1 Payoff | P2 Payoff |
|--------|--------|------|-----------|-----------|
| 2      | 2      | Yes | 2         | 2         |
| 2      | 3      | No (X<Y) | 2+2 = 4 | 2-2 = 0     |
| 2      | 4      | No (X<Y) | 4       | 0         |
| 2      | 5      | No (X<Y) | 4       | 0         |
| 3      | 2      | No (X>Y) | 2-2 = 0 | 2+2 = 4     |
| 3      | 3      | Yes | 3         | 3         |
| 3      | 4      | No (X<Y) | 3+2 = 5 | 3-2 = 1     |
| 3      | 5      | No (X<Y) | 5       | 1         |
| 4      | 2      | No (X>Y) | 2-2 = 0 | 2+2 = 4     |
| 4      | 3      | No (X>Y) | 3-2 = 1 | 3+2 = 5     |
| 4      | 4      | Yes | 4         | 4         |
| 4      | 5      | No (X<Y) | 4+2 = 6 | 4-2 = 2     |
| 5      | 2      | No (X>Y) | 2-2 = 0 | 2+2 = 4     |
| 5      | 3      | No (X>Y) | 3-2 = 1 | 3+2 = 5     |
| 5      | 4      | No (X>Y) | 4-2 = 2 | 4+2 = 6     |
| 5      | 5      | Yes | 5         | 5         |

So, we can compile the **payoff matrix for Player 1** (P1), where rows are P1's actions, columns are P2's actions:

```
       Y=2   Y=3   Y=4   Y=5
X=2:   2     4     4     4
X=3:   0     3     5     5
X=4:   0     1     4     6
X=5:   0     1     2     5
```

And for Player 2 (P2), from the same table:

```
       Y=2   Y=3   Y=4   Y=5
X=2:   2     0     0     0
```

### **Step 1: Understand the Game Mechanics**

We are in a **simultaneous two-player game**, where both players choose a probability distribution over four actions:

- A0 → 2  
- A1 → 3  
- A2 → 4  
- A3 → 5  

Each player picks a strategy (a probability distribution), and then a move is randomly drawn from each distribution. Let’s denote:
- Your move: X (from {2, 3, 4, 5})
- Opponent's move: Y (from {2, 3, 4, 5})

Payoffs for **Player 2 (you)**:
- If X = Y: you get X points.
- If X < Y: you get X + 2.0.
- If X > Y: you get Y - 2.0.

You are **Player 2** — this may matter only if strategies are asymmetric.

---

### **Step 2: Correctly Fill the Payoff Table for Player 2**

Let’s compute your payoff for each (X, Y):

| X \ Y | 2 (A0) | 3 (A1) | 4 (A2) | 5 (A3) |
|-------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| 2 (A0) | 2      | 2+2=4  | 2+2=4  | 2+2=4  |
| 3 (A1) | 2-2=0  | 3      | 3+2=5  | 3+2=5  |
| 4 (A2) | 2-2=0  | 3-2=1  | 4      | 4+2=6  |
| 5 (A3) | 2-2=0  | 3-2=1  | 4-2=2  | 5      |

So correct payoffs are as above.

Key observations:
- You get **6** only if X=4 and Y=5 → very high reward.
- You get **0** if X=3,4,5 and Y=2 → bad.
- If you play A0 (X=2), you get at least 2, and up to 4 against higher Y.
